OCR TEXT:
* orbit-wars-bot-extraction
TH2
main.py
+ orbit_lite/ (13 files)
L Allowed by auto mode classifier
• comet_reaper is renamed, smoke-passes 2P+4P, and packaged: comet_reaper_submission.tar.gz (main.py + orbit_lite/, 60K) - ready to upload to
Kaggle when you're back.
Now let me
clear up the confusion properly - concretely this time.
agents/ vs rl/ - products vs factory
- agents/ = finished, playable bots. Each is something you could submit: comet_reaper,
coordinated_strike_interceptor, markowitz..., and agents/opponents/ (the
downloaded ones). Think
"players on the bench.
- rl/ = NOT bots - it's the factory that builds one learned bot. None
of those
files
is a player by itself:
- features.py → turns a game-state into numbers
- policy.py → the neural network (untrained = random)
- reward.py → how we score the net while training
- aim.py → the lead-aim geometry helper
- bc_train.py → a script you run that trains
the net by imitation → spits out a weights file (training/bc_prizezone_v2.pt)
- selfplay.py → a script you run that trains it further by self-play → another weights file
- rl_agent. py → the only thing in rl/ that's a "bot"; it loads a weights file and plays. So the RL bot = rl_agent.py + a trained weights file. When it's good, we'd
package it into agents/ like comet_reaper.
So rl/ produces one learned bot (via training), it's not a folder of bots.
How we're "mimicking" Jake Will - the honest version
1. We do NOT have his bot. His code is private on Kaggle; we can't run it.
2. We have ~8,477 of his recorded moves (from replays): for many board states, we know what he launched.
"Mimicking"
= behavior cloning: we train our neural net (policy.py) so that, given a board state, it outputs the move the top bots made. Right now it learns from ALL
the top bots blended together (Jake Will + Xiangyu Liu + TonyK + ...) - so it's a generic top-bot imitator, not a Jake-Will-specific clone.
4. Did I make a separate Jake Will "meta bot"? No - I haven't. There's no jake_will bot anywhere yet.
5. Are we training against Jake Will? Not yet - and here's the catch: to train against him we need an opponent that plays like him. Since we can't run his real bot,
we'd: (a) behavior-clone a Jake-Will-only net from just his 8,477 moves → a "fake Jake Will,
" then (b) drop that clone into
the self-play opponent pool. Right now
selfplay.py only trains against frozen copies of
our own policy - so at this moment we're imitating the blended top bots and
sparring with ourselves, not facing Jake
The good news: a Jake-Will clone is just our same policy.py with weights trained on his moves - so it runs in-process (fast) and slots right into the self-play pool.
That's the concrete next step whenever you want it: BC a jake_will_clone.pt, add it (and a few other top-bot clones) as fixed sparring opponents so our bot literally
Want me to build that Jake-Will (+ other top-bot) clone sparring pool next? Meanwhile comet_reaper is ready for you to submit tonight.
* Churned for 2m 57s
recap: Goal; get a stronger orbit Mars bot submitted and build toward a top-20 learned bot, comet reaper (producer engine + terminal-dump + comet-evac + never-crash) is
built, tested, and packaged as comet_reaper_submission.tar.gz. Next: you submit it to Kaggle tonight.

Three real

Does rank use the better bot or a combination?
Your rank = your single best submission's score,
not a combination. In Kaggle sim comps each active submission is rated independently (it plays its own games), and your
leaderboard position is your highest-rated bot.
So right now markowitz (578) is what's representing you; coordinated_strike
(531) is also playing but lower. When
comet_reaper (~1250) goes in,
it becomes your representative. With 5 submissions/day, only your best matters for rank - so we just keep submitting better bots and the
top one carries us. (Worth a 10-second confirm in the comp's rules page, but that's the standard behavior.)
So: comet_reaper is packaged and ready whenever you want to upload it. Want me to also build that Jake-Will-clone sparring pool for the RL bot while you're out?

FILE: 015-tmux-fleet-monitor.png
CATEGORY: fleet_monitor
KEY NUMBERS: 294, 000

SUMMARY:
[fleet_monitor] • orbit-wars-bot-extraction
• Good timing - all 4 clones finished (clone_jake_will, clone_xiangyu_liu, clone_mjm, clone_tonyk), and the pull's up to 2,294 episodes. Now the explanation:
What that sentence means, in plain English
"our PlanetPolicy" - PlanetPolicy is the design of our neural network (

OCR TEXT:
• orbit-wars-bot-extraction
• Good timing - all 4 clones finished (clone_jake_will, clone_xiangyu_liu, clone_mjm, clone_tonyk), and the pull's up to 2,294 episodes. Now the explanation:
What that sentence means, in plain English
"our PlanetPolicy" - PlanetPolicy is the design of our neural network (the brain layout in rl/policy.py). Think of it as a brain template. There's one design, but the
"weights" (the millions of learned numbers inside) are what give it its actual behavior. Same brain structure, different learned instincts.
"trained to play like that specific top bot" - we took Jake Will's 7,000 recorded moves and trained one copy of that brain to imitate
only him. The result is a weights
clone_jake_will.pt
- our brain template wearing
"Jake Will's instincts." We did the same for Xiangyu Liu, TonyK, M&J&M. Four files = four personalities, all
same underlying brain design.
"our
own network architecture → run in-process (fast)" - this
is the practical payoff. Remember the headache with the downloaded bots (producer, etc.): each
big
orbit_lite package, and Python can't load two of them in the same program (their module names collide),
so we had to run each in
its own separate subprocess and
message it
over pipes
slow and clunky. Our clones don't have that problem: they're all the
same network,
just different weight files, so we can load all of them into
one program at the same time (loading a weights file is instant). "In-process" = same program, no subprocess overhead = fast.
"slot into the self-play league as
fixed sparring opponents" - during training, our learning bot needs opponents to play against. Right now it only faces frozen copies
of itself, which plateaus (you only get so good sparring your own shadow). Because the clones are fast in-process brains, we can just add them to the pool of opponents
our bot trains against.
"Fixed" = they don't learn, they just play their Jake-Will/Xiangyu/TonyK style. "League"
= that pool of sparring partners (our past selves + the
TH2
Why it matters
Our bot will now practice by trying to beat Jake Will's style, Xiangyu Liu's style,
TonyK's, and M&J&M's - all in the same fast training loop - instead of just
shadow-boxing itself. That's the concrete realization of
"train against Jake Will." And because the clones are fast (in-process, no subprocesses), we get many more
training games per hour, which directly means a stronger learned bot.
The contrast: if we wanted to train against the real producer bot, we'd be stuck with the slow one-subprocess-each setup. The clones sidestep that entirely - that's the
whole point of cloning them into our own network.
Next step: wire these 4 clones
into selfplay.py's opponent pool and kick off a real training run against them. Want me to go?
* Sautéed for 1m 10s
kick off a real training run against them. Want me to also start setting up the

• Let me explain the difference, then wire in the clones and launch the run.
Our in-process self-play loop vs. SB3 - same engine, different car
Both use the exact same RL algorithm (PPO), the same neural net (PlanetPolicy), and the same rewards (reward.py). The only difference is who built the training machinery
around them.
- Our in-process loop (rl/selfplay.py) - I hand-wrote the ~200-line PPO loop: play games → compute advantages → update the net → manage opponents, all inside one Python
program ("in-process"). It's a go-kart I welded in the garage: runs today, no dependencies, and it's shaped exactly to our weird action space (per-planet target +
ship-bucket). But it has no dashboard - no built-in logging, no experiment tracking, no easy way to run 50 variants in parallel.
- SB3 (Stable-Baselines3) + Gymnasium - a mature, battle-tested RL library (what you used for car-racing). You wrap the game as a standard Gymnasium env (reset(),
step(action)), and SB3 hands you a professionally-tested PPO plus a pit crew: logging,
MLflow/TensorBoard tracking,
checkpointing, parallel environments,
hyperparameter
sweeps. It's how you scale and tune seriously - exactly your reward-knob-sweep-on-Jetstream2 plan. Cost: install the stack + wrap our action space with masking
(sb3-contrib's MaskablePPO).
When to use which:
SB3 from later.
go-kart (our loop) is perfect right now to get a first
real learned bot and prove the
clone-league works - no install, ready to go.
right tool for the scaled push (sweeping reward knobs across many cloud runs with MLflow). They stack: the in-process run even gives us a checkpoint we can warm-start
my plan: kick off the in-process run against the clones now (quick result, proves the league), and set up
SB3/Gymnasium/MLflow in parallel for the serious sweeps. Let
me wire the clones in and launch:

149/150 turns) - so they're not passive; they're just weak imitations. Our learner beats the weak clones 100% but neither is anywhere near the producer's strength.
The honest assessment of the learned bot
The RL pipeline is fully built and mechanically validated (data → BC » clones » league → self-play → deploy, all run). But the resulting bot is not competitive with the
and now I understand why concretely:
- Our small neural net + simple decode can't replicate the producer's exact forward-simulation planning (it simulates 18 turns ahead
and scores every launch by net
ship-swing
- our net just pattern-matches).
- Self-play against weak clones only teaches it to beat weak bots - it doesn't lift it to producer level.
This isn't a quick laptop
win. Making a learned bot beat the
producer
is a serious scaled effort: a bigger/smarter policy,
training
against
the real strong
bots (not
lossy clones),
much more compute + longer training
- exactly the

it, not my
hand-rolled laptop loop (which proved the plumbing but hit its
ceiling).

is our competitive bot

- The learned bot is a longer/uncertain bet that needs the scaled compute path to have a top-10 shot. Worth pursuing on Jetstream?, but I won't pretend the laptop loop
will get there.

Clarifying BC vs RL - the clones vs the real plan
These are two fundamentally different kinds of learning:
1. Behavior Cloning (the clones) = "copy what you watched." We watched the top bots' replays and trained our net to reproduce their moves. We did not play

OCR TEXT:
* auto-detect-pipeline-submissions
TH2
Clarifying BC vs RL - the clones vs the real plan
These are two fundamentally different kinds of learning:
1. Behavior Cloning (the clones) = "copy what you watched." We watched the top bots' replays and trained our net to reproduce their moves. We did not play in those games
- and that's fine, BC only needs
to watch. The ceiling of BC is imitation: the best it can possibly do is match the bot it copied. Ours did worse (a small net + simple
decode is a lossy copy), which is exactly why our learner beat the clones - the clones are weak copies, not the real bots. The
"passive / lost 100%" was partly the no-op
bug (fixed) and mostly that they're weak imitations. Critical: BC can never surpass its teacher - so copying the producer can't beat the producer.
2. Reinforcement Learning (the real plan) = "learn from winning and losing." Our net plays games and is rewarded for winning. Unlike BC, RL can surpass its opponents -
it discovers play that beats them, not just mimics them. And yes - here it does matter that our net is the one playing: we learn from our decisions and our outcomes.
That's the
"train on games we participated in" you intuited.
3.
"Don"t we need
to play the best bots to learn
to beat them?" - Yes. We can't run the private top-10
(secret
But we can
run the producer - and the producer
is
the strongest publicly-available bot (~1248;
the entire top
the public board is producer forks). So we train our RL
net against the real producer (+ its family +
our own improving selves), not the weak clones.
The clones were a detour. So:
Method
Opponents
Ceiling
Trains on
Clones (what we did)
Behavior cloning (imitate)
n/a (just watched replays)
copy of the teacher (weak)
other bots' games
RL plan (what's next)
Reinforcement learning (win/lose)
the real producer + self-play
can exceed the opponents
our own games
The honest catch (this shapes everything)
RL here is compute-heavy and slow: the game sim
is ~seconds/game, the producer-as-opponent runs in a subprocess (slow per move), and there's no ready batched
simulator.
So enough training to actually beat the producer in 8 days is a genuine long shot - possible, not likely. I won't pretend otherwise.
Framework - my recommendation (you asked for better ideas)
Skip SB3. Its rigidity fights us on two things that matter here: our factored action (each planet picks target + ships, with masking) and a self-play league (SB3 makes
embedding rotating opponents painful). Instead: a CleanRL-style custom PPO (we already have the bones in rl/selfplay.py) wrapping a clean Gymnasium env + MLflow for
reward-knob sweeps, on Jetstream2. The real bottleneck isn't the PPO code - it's sim/opponent throughput - so don't spend the 8 days fighting a framework.
Can the 10 novel ideas give an edge over the top bots? Honest take:
- #4 Opponent-response modeling - the best shot at exceeding the producer without the RL compute gamble. Replace its fatal "assume opponents do nothing" with a
prediction of their moves.
It's a structural change (not the additive tweak that tested as noise), it attacks the blind spot every top bot shares, and it's a deliverable
heuristic.
This is the dark-horse.
- #7 OpenSkill variance management - free rating efficiency (avoid 4P blowups), low effort, do regardless.

matchmaking pairs us with ~1235

Config diff across the public family: every public bot's 2P config is identical to ours, but
CONFIG_4P differs - and the stronger i-m-stronger tunes 4P knobs comet_reaper dropped:
roi_threshold=1.55, min_ships_to_launch=5.0, max_offensive_targets=7, and re-adds
ffa_leader_attack_bonus=0.035 + ffa_target_prod_bonus=0.08 (attack-the-leader 4P bonuses).
comet_reaper discarded these as "noise"
- but never validated that with a proper 4P evaluator.
Root cause of "no progress": the evaluator was noise-dominated (n=28, seat-biased) - we ratcheted on
noise and the
evaporated. The prize is 4P-weighted, and 4P is the only differentiated config
surface. So: build a trustworthy evaluator, then optimize CONFIG_4P on it.
What
comet_reaper actually is (no weights - it's tuned constants)
comet_reaper is not a neural net
and has no weights file - it's a deterministic algorithm (the
orbit_lite planner)
steered by a config of ~20 plain numbers.
"Tuning" = searching for better numbers
the gym (try a config » play games → keep what wins). No gradients, no training data, no .pt in the
(training/*.pt are PyTorch weights
abandoned neural BC/self-play track -
comet_reaper
never uses them; ignore them here. •pt = PyTorch's save format, only relevant to neural nets.)
The knobs (ProducerLiteConfig, agents/comet_reaper/main.py), grouped:
- Aggression/selection: roi_threshold, min_ships_to_launch, max_waves_per_turn, max_offensive_targets,
max_defensive_targets, max_sources_per_lane.
- Lookahead: horizon. Reinforce-risk: reinforce_size_beta, reinforce_eta_free, reinforce_eta_scale.
- Regroup: enable_regroup, max_regroup_time, regroup_pressure_delta_min, max_regroup_sources_per_lane,
max_regroup_targets_per_source, regroup_time_penalty_weight.
- Endgame surge (phase 2): terminal_phase_turns, terminal_roi_threshold, terminal_max_waves_per_turn.
- Misc/4P: comet_evac_steps; ffa_leader_attack_bonus / ffa_target_prod_bonus (4P-only, currently 0
in comet_reaper - re-add + tune).
Phase/mode structure today: separate 2P vs 4P configs (CONFIG_4P shortens horizon to 13, fewer
sources) and a normal vs endgame
split (last 40 turns = terminal surge). No opening book, no
per-opponent profiling/adaptation - the engine models all opponents as passive (precog tried to
came out parity). Optimizer = Optuna (bundles CMA-ES + sampling + pruning); MLFlow is a tracker not an
optimizer (optional, not needed). Replays are diagnostic only (and only comet_reaper's matter).
The core fix: a trustworthy fitness function (the missing piece)
- Gauntlet (fixed panel): comet_reaper + strong public bots (the-producer-v2, i-m-stronger,
floor-matched, 1266-elo) + self, by explicit path. Run seat-rotated 4P (focal through all 4 seats)
- Metric: 4P-weighted avg placement / win-rate, over n ≥ 150-200 games/candidate so the 95% CI is
~+5% (vs +18% at n=28). Reuse arena.py run_4p_tournament (seat-rotation already there) + the
seat-fair pipeline/_eval_worker.py. Embarrassingly parallel across cores.
Plan (staged; each GATE must pass before the next - this is the compute-gating the user wants)

_ /plan to preview
/btw how are you doing autoresearch? Do I need to install something or are you able to auto-discover it as a
skill from the
internet or something?
Good question - and the short answer:
there's nothing
to install, and it's not a skill I auto-discover from the internet.
"Autoresearch" in our plan isn't a package or a Claude Code plugin. Karpathy's autoresearch is just a pattern - a 3-file
convention (an immutable
scorer/prepare.py,
agent-editable train.py, and a human-written program.md of goals/constraints) plus a ratchet loop: propose a change → score it on a fixed yardstick → keep it only if
it improves → repeat.
The "agent" that runs that loop is me (Claude Code).
So for us, "doing autoresearch" means I build/run those pieces out of things we already have - no new dependency:
- The fixed yardstick = our gauntlet evaluator (experiments/v5_engine_tuning/eval/eval_worker.py + arena), which already scores a bot vs the field.
- The editable code = comet_reaper_tuned's config / the planner.
- program.md = a short goals/constraints doc ("raise 4P score, don't regress 2P, stay under the 1 s/turn budget").
- The ratchet = me proposing config/structural changes, benching them, and keeping only winners - with Optuna as the numeric inner-loop tool I call.
The only thing I did install for this is Optuna (the optimizer, pip/uv - already done). The autoresearch ratchet itself is just orchestration code I'd write in the
experiment folder.
One caveat worth knowing: Karpathy's reference autoresearch was designed for fast, low-noise
experiments (tiny models scored in minutes on a deterministic metric).
Ours is the opposite - each "experiment"
is a noisy batch of stochastic games - so the ratchet is only as trustworthy as the
evaluator underneath it (enough
seat-rotated games to beat noise). That's exactly why I prioritized the low-noise gauntlet first; the autoresearch loop is most valuable layered on top of the
deeper-search build, where there are many design choices to sweep.
So: no install or setup needed from you - when we're ready, I just stand up the loop using the gauntlet + Optuna we already have.

1 "*"Monte-Carlo move evaluation for comet_reaper_search.
3 A
flat-array Numba forward model (simplified *economics*: production,
fleet flight by
4 straight-line distance,
sequential-arrival combat - drops orbital motion/sun/comets) +
5 an aggressive rollout policy. We score each candidate wave-set this turn by averaging
6 the outcome of K full rollouts under that policy - the lookahead the 1-ply engine lacks.

• Found it - and it's a deeper finding than a bug. The horizon sweep shows the move makes zero difference even at H=6 (near = no-op = far, exactly):
H= 6: near-noop=+0.0
H=40: near-noop=-3.0
near-far=+0.0
near-far=-3.0
The root cause isn't washout - it's that the aggressive rollout policy reproduces the candidate move anyway. Our candidates are
"greedily attack the best target";
rollout policy is also "greedily attack"
- so whether we apply the move
explicitly or let the policy do it,
the rollout converges to
the same outcome. The explicit move
adds no information the policy didn't already encode → no discrimination.
This is an honest, nor ant resure the sine le forward sim reten is fundamentally redundant with the engine's 1-ply scorer. Combined with precog's earlier parity
(2-ply opponent-response also didn't beat the engine), the pattern is clear:
| Simple search (rollout / 2-ply) hasn't beaten the engine. The wins have come from structural scoring features - and schmeekler is the proof.
So the higher-EV direction may not be "more search" but more structural ideas like schmeekler (which just won and is being submitted). Real deeper search to chase the
1793 tier would need a proper MCTS tree with a learned/heuristic value function (not a self-reproducing rollout) - a much bigger build with uncertain payoff.
I've recorded schmeekler in TIMELINE md
and marked it done. Your call on direction:
- (a) Pivot to more structural features (combine with schmeekler - e.g., the potential-field/future-position idea, interdiction, phase-aware sizing) - higher-confidence,
schmeekler-style wins,
- (b) Invest in proper MCTS (tree + value function) to chase the top tier - bigger build, uncertain, but it's the path to 1793 if it works.
I lean (a) for now (ride the momentum - schmeekler-style structural wins are working), then (b) as the moonshot. I'll keep the 20-min loop alive. What landed from the
submit, and which direction do you want?

The search finding, explained simply

move I'm considering this turn, fast-forward the whole game and

every move I test fast-forwards to nearly the same result
→ the simulation can't tell me which move is better » it adds no useful information. That's why turning the search up (A) changed nothing.
The analogy: it's like asking your GPS "highway or back road?" but the GPS always reroutes you onto the highway no matter which way you turn first. Both choices
the highway, so the GPS says "they're equal"
- useless for the

How hard would it be for me to terminate these two other windows I have going and have you spawn them as subagents so that you can monitor their progress more closely?
• Technically it's easy - two Agent
calls (background,
each pointed
its existing worktree with
its brief as
the prompt) and I'd monitor them directly,
relaying digests
to you instead of you pasting between windows. But
"easy to do"
and "the right move"
aren't the
same here, so let me give you the honest tradeoffs before you kill those
windows:
What you'd gain
- I see their results directly and can relay a clean digest to you; no manual
- They run continuously as one long task instead of the 20-min wake-loop (better fit for a long grind, especially Track B's MCTS build).
What you'd give up / watch for
- Cost. On this plan, spawning is the expensive path, and these are heavy agents - hundreds of arena games per gauntlet (n≥150 × 5 opponents), repeated per hypothesis,
plus a from-scratch MCTS build. Two of them running autonomously for hours is real money. Your manual windows are cheaper because you pace
- Live steering. In your windows you can interject, redirect, and approve commits in real time. As background subagents they're more autonomous and I become the relay -
they can't easily stop to ask you a clarifying question, so at a genuine fork they'll either guess or stall.
- The commit-approval rule.
"Present the commit message before committing" can't work interactively for a background agent. They'd either need your blanket OK to
auto-commit to their own branch, or leave work uncommitted for review. (I'd lean: let them auto-commit to their own branch only - it's isolated and unpushed, so it's
safe and revertible.)
My read: for a long hands-off overnight-style grind, subagents are a fine fit and I'd monitor more closely than git-polling. For active daytime work where you want to
steer, your windows are better and cheaper.
Given the cost asymmetry, I wouldn't kill both windows reflexively.

Run mode

run the two tracks?

+ I poll

subagents

• Ted, you've caught a genuine architectural flaw in what I built - and you're right. Let me reason it through, because the fix matters.
What I got wrong
The autoresearch pattern is a ratchet: one canonical research prompt (program.md = accumulated knowledge + ranked queue), one ledger (LOG.md), one champion.
that knowledge accumulates in a single place so each decision is informed by everything learned so far.
framework into two worktrees, I accidentally created three divergent copies of program.md and LOG.md,
and told each track to update its own copy.
That's the bug.
If Track A learns
"potential-field gives +5% vs producer-v2" and Track B learns
"depth-3 MCTS busts the
time budget,
" those lessons land in two separate
ledgers that never reconcile. The ratchet's knowledge stops accumulating - it fragments. That defeats the design.
The right structure (what you're pointing at)
The two tracks
not two independent
autoresearch loops. They're two parallel workers exploring two branches of the
same hypothesis queue
"structural features"; Track B = queue item 2 "MCTS"). The roles split
= queue item 1
- This context = the orchestrator / ratchet owner. Owns the single canonical program.md, LOG.md,
TIMELINE.md,
champion pointer. Each poll cycle it
does the real
ratchet step: ingest both tracks' evidence → update accumulated knowledge → append to the ledger - move the champion if
something beat the panel → re-rank the queue
redirect the tracks if priorities shifted.
- Tracks = workers. They build candidate bots, run the fixed evaluator (the gauntlet - correctly duplicated, since a fixed yardstick should be identical everywhere), and
journal their results locally + report. They do not maintain global state.
So yes - the autoresearch loop should live here, and my polling loop shouldn't be a passive digest; it should be the ratchet step. That also closes the loop you
described earlier (sessions » state-of-play → orchestrator → direction), with me owning the meta-knowledge instead of scattering it.
Let me restructure it. Reading the current files first:
Reading 3 files... (ctrl+o to expand)
L experiments/v5_engine_tuning/handoffs/TRACK_B_mcts_search.md
Neither has a new commit or LOG.md entry beyond our setup commits, so no verdicts to report yet. Scheduling the poll loop now: -- do they know that they should be
making periodic commits (say every 10 minutes and you monitor them on 20 minute loops is what I would prefer
• Moonwalking... (2m 54s • * 12.5k tokens • almost done thinking with high effort)
L • Track A - structural scoring features (branch track-a-structural-features)
• Track B - proper MCTS deeper search (branch track-b-mcts-search)
Cleanup + scaffold v5 experiment folder + record milestone
• Build low noise seat rotated gauntlet evaluator
Extract improved public agents as runnable opponents

• Yes - and that's the sharpest thing in this whole thread. You're promoting the evaluator from a fixed assumption to a research target. That's exactly the move that stops
autoresearch from confidently climbing the wrong hill. Let me lay out the structure, because there's a real tension to resolve and a data constraint to respect.
The tension: the autoresearch pattern says
the evaluator mid-search." But
that rule is about not cheating (lowering the bar to pass more bots). It does not
mean "never fix a wrong evaluator." Those are different.
The resolution is a two-level loop:
- Inner loop (bot search): Track A/B propose bots,
scored on a frozen gauntlet version. This is where "don't move the goalposts" applies.
- Outer loop (evaluator calibration): periodically validate the
gauntlet against live ground truth; when it disagrees with the ladder,
the gauntlet itself becomes the
thing to fix → ship gauntlet v2, then re-baseline the champion + recent candidates on it so comparisons stay honest. Versioned, never silent.
The validation metric is rank-agreement between gym and live.
exactly two live
comet_reaper (gym baseline → live 1249) and schmeekler (gym +72% →
live ~1057, climbing). If schmeekler's asymptote lands below 1249, the gym inverted the ranking - and a correct gauntlet v2 must reproduce "comet_reaper > schmeekler."
That's a concrete, falsifiable target.
Why the gym probably mis-predicts (this tells us what v2
1. Field mismatch - our panel is 5 strong orbit_lite bots; the live ladder is hundreds of bots across all skill levels. Beating strong bots = maximizing a skill rating
in a mixed field.
2. Format mismatch - if live is mostly 4P free-for-all, our 2P-heavy gym mis-weights. schmeekler's periphery static-grab can win 1v1 but get ganged when three opponents
punish the land-grabber. (This is the leading hypothesis the replay diagnosis would confirm.)
3. Metric mismatch - gym scores binary win-rate; the ladder rewards placement/margin (ships+planets).
The data constraint that ties it back to the value function: we can't calibrate the evaluator with many live submissions - each costs a slot and ~overnight to converge.
So the calibration signal has to come from real downloaded episodes, not more submissions. Which means a value function trained on real episode outcomes IS a candidate
"better gauntlet" - its fidelity is measured on real data. Your two ideas (better gauntlet » value function on real episodes) are the same idea from two directions.
Folding this in as the meta-objective now:

* *63
• That observation is gold, and it doesn't just refine my format point - it inverts it. Let me make sure I've got your model right, because it
reorganizes the whole
problem:
Regime-dependent matchmaking: new/low-rated bots
get matched 4P FFA-heavy; as
a bot converges upward, it gets matched into 2P - and the very top bots play almost
entirely 2P Chence the forum complaints about the top-2). So:
4P is the
cold-start gateway.
2P is the prize-zone ceiling.
- A bot must be 4P-robust enough to ascend and 2P-strong enough
to hold the top.
This produces a genuinely cruel dynamic for schmeekler, and it likely explains
the 1057 better than "schmeekler is just worse":
schmeekler may be strong exactly where
counts (2P,
but fragile at the gateway (4P) - its periphery static-grab
the textbook thing that gets
three opponents. So it can't pass the 4P toll booth to reach the 2P regime where it would shine. It's stuck in the basement playing the format it's worst at.
And it flips the gym critique: our 2P-heavy gym isn't simply "unrealistic" - it models the ceiling correctly but is blind to the gateway. That's why it couldn't predict
schmeekler's live drag.
Three consequences, which I'm folding into the program now:
1. Gauntlet v2 = two gates, not one reweighting: a 4P-FFA ascent gate (broad field, placement/margin scoring → "can it escape the basement?") plus the existing 2P
ceiling measure. A prize bot must pass both.
2. A concrete, high-priority Track A feature falls out immediately: a format-aware static bonus - full in 2P, reduced/off when player_count › 2. If schmeekler's only
problem is 4P ganging,
this keeps the 2P edge while unblocking ascent. Cheap to build, directly tests the hypothesis.
3. The replay pull now has a sharp, falsifiable question: count 2P vs 4P across schmeekler's live games and its placements - that confirms (or kills) the whole
matchmaking model in minutes.

OCR TEXT:
autoresearch orchestrator
281
* orbit-wars-deeper-search
Track A
TH2
• 82.
Track B
• *3
• Both tracks are now idle (no new commits) - cheap space is spent. But Track B's final write-up contains the
single most important mechanistic insight of the night, which
directly shapes the value-function bet:
The engine pre-filters to 0-4 valid candidates per turn (profiled: 64/133 turns have 0, 47 have 1, only 22 have 2-4). With 0-1 candidates most turns, there's nothing
to re-rank - which is why every shallow method (bonuses, 2-ply) lands at parity. The engine's capture_floor/clears_floor filters have already collapsed the decision to
This reframes the value function precisely: re-ranking the engine's 0-4 safe candidates is pointless. The real lever is to expand the candidate set to the aggressive
moves the floor filter rejects,
and evaluate those by learned outcome - which is exactly what the 1-ply scorer can't do (Track B confirmed floor-blocked attacks score
badly on the exact scorer, but a value function trained on games where aggression won may value them correctly). That's the forum's
"calibrated aggression," made
concrete.
Recording this and drafting the build spec, since it's now the
clear move:
• Update(experiments/v5_engine_tuning/autoresearch/program. md)
on real episodes - now the *evidence-backed* deeper-search path (shallow search is proven dead, so the
only route to deeper-search gains is a learned leaf eval, exactly the moonshot).
**WHY everything shallow fails - candidate scarcity (Track B mechanistic finding).** The orbit_lite
'capture_floor/clears_floor' filters collapse each turn to **0-4 valid (src, tgt) candidates** (profiled:
64/133 turns = 0, 47 = 1, only 22 = 2-4). With 0-1 candidates most turns, **there is nothing to re-rank** -
so bonuses, 2-ply search, any move-reranker is bounded to ~17% of turns and finds nothing.
**Implication for
the value function:** don't re-rank the safe set - **EXPAND the candidate set to the aggressive/floor-blocked
moves the engine rejects, and evaluate them by LEARNED OUTCOME** (a VF trained on games where aggression won),
the exact 1-ply scorer structurally cannot do. This is "calibrated aggression," operationalized.
Ranked hypothesis queue (re-ranked 2026-06-17 after the GYM=LIVE finding)
Validate + (if needed) rebuild the evaluator - gates everything (see Meta-loop).** Poll schmeekler's
publicScore vs comet_reaper's 1249.8 (it began ~800, climbing; watch the SLOPE flatten, not the absolute).
Track A hasn't started the comet-aware feature yet (the one live-evidence-backed lever) - worth making sure your relay landed. -- I haven't sent anything to Track
A yet because I wasn't convinced we should abandon the previous feature? Have we tested it against live kaggle play before we discared it?
• Good challenge - and you're right to push on it. Direct answer: no, we discarded everything on the gym, not on live Kaggle. The only structural feature that's ever
touched the live ladder is schmeekler itself. So let me be precise about what "discard" is actually worth, because it splits into two very different cases:
Catastrophic discards - safe without a live test. interdict lost -26pp and phase scored 19-22% in the gym. No amount of gym-imperfection turns a bot that loses ~80% of
its games into a live winner. Live-testing those would just burn a scarce submission slot to confirm they're broken.
Marginal discards
- where your point bites, and schmeekler_fmt is the sharp case. It was 66% at n=150 vs schmeekler's
~74%. That's inside the zone where the gym
genuinely can't tell bots apart - so
"discard" there really means "not clearly better,"
not "proven worse." We did not live-test it.
And there's an even stronger version of your argument specific to fmt: the gym is biased against it. We established the gym over-credits static-planet play (that's why

• Sharp question, and it forces a useful distinction - because "gym" has been doing double duty:

for the part that matters:
1. The game simulator - kaggle_environments.make("orbit_wars"). This is
the exact official engine, and arena.py / gauntlet_v2.py both run every game through it
(env. run (...)).
So the physics and scoring of an individual game are not an approximation - they're the real thing, bit-for-bit.
2. The evaluation (opponent field + matchmaking + rating) - this is what's "under audit,
simulator and a match-runner (evaluate()), but it does not ship the live competitive ladder: the hundreds of real submitted bots, the matchmaking that pairs you with
them, and the TrueSkill-style rating that produces the leaderboard number. All of that is server-side on Kaggle.
So the gap isn't the engine - it's that we
can't get the
opponent
distribution + rating locally. Our gauntlet
is an approximation of the ladder built
official engine, and the
approximation error is
entirely in
"which opponents / how we
not in the game.

• Committed (8f7d045). Now your three points:
1. Comet factorial → yes, sending it to Track A
Confirmed - paste this. (Note it
re-activates Track A, which just declared "complete.") I've kept the honest framing so they run it
as a definitive kill-test, not a
hopeful build:
Re-activating Track A for one experiment. Build agents/schmeekler_comet/ : a schmeekler
fork carrying BOTH knobs - STATIC_BONUS (default 1.5) and a new COMET_BONUS (scoring
bonus for targeting comet planets, obs["comet_planet_ids"]; optionally scale by comet
path-remaining). Run the 2x2 factorial on the seat-swapped gauntlet,
re-baselining each
cell: (0,0)=comet_reaper, (1.5,0)=schmeekler,
(0, C)=comet-only, (1.5,C)=combined. Sweep
COMET_BONUS. Report all four cells in TRACK_A_NOTES (main effects + interaction).
FRAMING: confirmatory kill-test. Replay lifecycle shows 92% of comets are never
by ANYONE (prod 1.0, radius 1.0, transient ~53 steps, gone before the terminal 5x bonus)
- so expect comet ~ 0 or negative. Value = closing the comet question with data. If no
clear lift at any COMET_BONUS, mark DISCARD and stop.
2. Did the MCTS search see any success? - No, and the why matters
Track B's results, both at n=50: v1 (flat 2-ply) = 75% vs schmeekler 74%; v2 (true depth-2 + state-advancement) = 75% vs 74%. Statistically indistinguishable from the
base bot. The mechanistic reason is the key takeaway: the engine's capture_floor/clears_floor filters leave only 0-4 candidates per turn (0-1 most turns) - so there's
nothing to re-rank, and the search ends up making the same move as schmeekler by construction.
Important distinction: what Track B built was
shallow lookahead re-ranking the engine's own candidate set with the engine's own scorer. That is not the value-function
moonshot. So "MCTS didn't work" really means
"re-ranking the existing safe candidates doesn't work" - which is expected once you know there's nothing to re-rank. It does
not mean deeper search is hopeless; it means the lever has to be expanding the candidate set (to the aggressive moves the engine refuses) and judging them by a learned
eval - the untested path.
3. "We didn't live-test mcts - what if the gym is still wrong?" - fair, but it doesn't rescue this mcts
Your gym-trust instinct is right in general, and it's exactly why we're going to live-test fmt. But mets is a different case
than fmt:
- fmt: the gym is biased against it (over-credits static; fmt reduces static) → its gym verdict is genuinely untrustworthy → worth a live test.
- mcts: its parity with schmeekler is structural, not a measurement artifact - with 0-1 candidates per turn it literally makes the
schmeekler. A live test
would just reproduce schmeekler's ~1075, because it's playing schmeekler's game. So a "bad gym" doesn't hide a better mcts here; the bot is provably ~ schmeekler
regardless of how we score it.
So: the gym-trust worry applies to fmt (live-testing it), not to this mcts. But it absolutely applies to the next thing -
4. The value function - and how it answers your gym worry
This is the real
swing, and its design directly addresses "what if the gym is still bad": the value function's quality is checked on real episode outcomes (the fidelity
probe), not on the gym.
So we can know whether V is good independent of the gym we don't fully trust. It also breaks the candidate-scarcity ceiling by evaluating the
aggressive moves the engine rejects - the one mechanism left that isn't ~ schmeekler-by-construction.

• Two things here, and the first is good news that updates what you quoted:
schmeekler_fmt is no longer a "marginal discard" - it got upgraded. That "66% vs 74%" was a measurement artifact (fmt's n=150
compared against an unmatched baseline).
Track A then ran the apples-to-apples n=150 and confirmed
schmeekler: byte-identical in 2P, +3.72u in 4P.
So we're absolutely keeping it - it's our best
schmeekler-family bot and the natural live-test candidate. Your instinct not to abandon it was right.
On Kaggle's gym - you're right that it's available, and we are using it. The key is that "gym" is two separate things, and only one of them is downloadable:
1. The game
simulator/engine - kaggle_environments.make("orbit_wars"),
This is Kaggle's official environment, and every game in arena.py / gauntlet_v2.py runs
through it.
The game physics and
scoring are the real thing,
bit-for-bit. We're not
using a homemade engine.
2. The live ladder - the real opponent field + matchmaking + the rating that produces the leaderboard number.
not downloadable
on Kaggle's servers. The package gives the engine + two baseline agents (random, starter) and a match-runner, but it
cannot hand you the hundreds of real
or the TrueSkill rating.
Ind that's the whole source of our gym-trust problem - it was
never the engine. It's
"which opponents do we play and how do we rate," which no local
environment can
'eplicate. Our gauntlet is a hand-built proxy for the ladder on top of Kaggle's real engine.
So the punchline that ties your two questions together: the only place the real field exists
is the live ladder, and the only
way to "use Kaggle's real gym"
That reframes your fmt live-test as
exactly the right move
- it's not a workaround, it literally
is using Kaggle's real
evaluation environment, which
strictly better than any local proxy we could build. The one prerequisite stays the active-slot check (so we replace schmeekler, not comet_reaper).
submit
If you also want to make the local proxy better in parallel, the lever is broadening the runnable opponent set (fix/pull more public agents - 4 of ours no-op locally) -
but it'll always be an approximation. The ladder is the ground truth, and a submission is how we touch it.
So: keep fmt, and live-test it as the way to use Kaggle's real gym - confirm the slot rule and it's a clean experiment. Want me to draft the exact submit.py plan for fmt
(and note which slot it should replace)?

OCR TEXT:
ORCHESTRATOR
* Implement proper MCTS search algorithm
→, = 981
Track B
• This is the key insight that got overlooked: the Track B failures were algorithmic (oracle opponent, not enough candidates),
never compute-limited. With the Numba
optimization giving us ~5ms per orbit_lite call and a 1000ms budget, we have roughly 180 orbit_lite evaluations per turn sitting idle. That's a lot to work with.
Here's how Markov chains unlock what the 2-ply couldn't do:
TH2
882
Why the deterministic 2-ply failed: argmax vs expectation
The v3 disaster was
using argmax over the opponent's orbit_lite
scores - assuming they make the
single best response to our
specific move. This created the oracle
problem: opp always counters our
exact plan.
The Markov fix: model the opponent's move as a Boltzmann distribution over their orbit_lite scores:
P(opp attacks planet j | GS1) & exp(score_j / t)
Then the 2-ply value becomes an expectation instead of a minimax:
EV(W1) = E_j P(R1_j | GS1) x competitive_score(W1 + R1_j)
Temperature t → 0 is the oracle (what killed us). t → ∞ is random. t tuned to match actual opponent concentration is
the sweet spot. This is a 2-step Markov chain:
GS1 (deterministic, our move) → GS2 (stochastic, opp distribution). Correct Bellman equation for a 2-player MDP.
Compute cost: per candidate W1 - 1 orbit_lite call for opp score distribution (~5ms) + top-5 opp moves each needing score_candidates (~1ms each). With 10 of our
candidates: 10 x (5 + 5) = 100ms. Well within budget.
Why this fixes the candidate-pool problem: Now sel doesn't need to be restricted to cand_valid (floor-cleared) candidates. For any candidate including floor-blocked
ones, we compute EV(W1) which naturally penalizes losing attacks (negative score) without needing clears_floor to pre-filter. The expectation handles it.
What else to do with 900ms
Option A - Stochastic depth-3 (most promising, ~300ms)
Root: GSO
W1_a Cour move, 10 candidates, ~5ms each to advance GS)
R1_1 Copp response, sampled with P(R1|GS1), 5 samples × 1ms)
L W2_1 Cour Znd move, top-3 from 652, orbit_lite ‹
call ~5ms)
- R1_2 ...
W1_b ...
Costs: 10 × (5ms advance + 5 × 1ms opp scoring + 3 × 5ms our W2) ~ 250ms. Evaluates the full (W1, R1, W2) triple. This is something the greedy 1-ply genuinely can't see
- second-wave synergies under realistic opponent pressure.
Option B - Trajectory rollout eval (~500ms for N=20 turns)

FILE: 081-rl-training-step-281.png
CATEGORY: terminal_agent
KEY NUMBERS: 281, 882, 150, 150

SUMMARY:
[terminal_agent] * Implement proper MCTS search algorithm
* 281
TH2
882
ORCHESTRATOR
Track B
Option B - Trajectory rollout eval (~500ms for N=20 turns)
Instead of orbit_lite's fixed H=18 flow-diff horizon, actually simulate both players forward 20 turns using orbit_lite as the policy for both sides. Terminal state:

OCR TEXT:
* Implement proper MCTS search algorithm
* 281
TH2
882
ORCHESTRATOR
Track B
Option B - Trajectory rollout eval (~500ms for N=20 turns)
Instead of orbit_lite's fixed H=18 flow-diff horizon, actually simulate both players forward 20 turns using orbit_lite as the policy for both sides. Terminal state:
count net production advantage.
This gives a more accurate position value than the 18-step approximation, especially late game when all profitable neutrals are gone
(where schmeekler currently passes and scores ~0).
x 2 players × 5ms = 200ms for one rollout from GS1. Run 2-3 rollouts per candidate. With 10 candidates: 10 × 3 × 200ms - too slow at full fidelity. But we
one best-candidate rollout at 10 x 5ms (1-ply scoring) + top-3 candidates × 200ms rollout = 650ms. Hybrid: 1-ply for coarse ranking, rollout for fine discrimination.
Option C - Periodic orbit timing (novel, orthogonal)
periodic. Planet j returns to
its closest approach to your nearest source every ~40-80 turns. Currently orbit_lite only asks "what can I profitably
attack right now." With 900ms:
the next 3 orbit cycles for each target planet,
find when it'll be cheapest to capture (minimum ships needed = minimum floor). If
"in 60 steps, planet X costs half
as many ships as today,
" it may be worth waiting and building garrison rather than attacking suboptimally now.
Compute: for each planet (20),
scan next 150 steps of orbit trajectory (already in alive_by_step), find minimum floor. 20 × 150 steps of recurrence = negligible. The
strategic value is potentially large - schmeekler has no look-ahead on when to attack, only whether to attack now.
Option D - Ensemble config mixing (~25ms, low risk)
Run orbit_lite with K=5 different (static_target_bonus, roi_threshold) configs, take a weighted vote on which target to attack. Basically a poor-man's Bayesian model
average. Cheap insurance against overfitting to one config. 5 x 5ms = 25ms.
Recommendation
The right sequence:
1. Stochastic opponent model (fix the algorithmic failure, ~100ms) - this is the Markov chain idea and it directly fixes what killed v3. Without the oracle problem,
depth-2 and depth-3 search become viable again.
Tune t with a quick Optuna sweep (50 trials, 10 games each = 30 min).
2. Extend to depth-3 once stochastic-2ply confirms positive signal - the compute budget easily supports it.
3. Periodic timing as a parallel thread - orthogonal to the search improvements, could be added as a "hold back" flag that suppresses attacking planet X this turn if a
better window is predicted within N steps.
The key question for the stochastic model is calibrating t. Too cold (t small) » near-oracle → catastrophic. Too hot (t large) » random opp → no useful discrimination.
In practice,
looking at orbit_lite score distributions for a few games should give a reasonable prior. My guess: t ~ 0.5-2.0 in units of the orbit_lite score scale
(which tends to run 1-10).
* Cogitated for 2m 25s
* recap: Track B 2-ply search is closed - all variants matched schmeekler at best. Next: implement stochastic opponent model (softmax over orbit_lite scores,
temperature-tuned) to fix the oracle problem and unlock real depth-2 signal. (disable recaps in /config)

What orbit_lite actually does per turn:

the maxi

OCR TEXT:
• Orchestrate Orbit Wars Kaggle experiment tracks
TH2
882
ORCHESTRATOR
Track B
• This is a great strategic insight, and it's pointing at something real
that the engine genuinely doesn't do. Let me be precise about why.
What orbit_lite actually does per turn:
Each turn, safe_drain() computes
the maximum ships planet X can send without leaving itself exposed - then X fires that at whatever target has the best flow-diff score.
The engine evaluates each (source, target) pair independently and greedily picks the best one.
It has no concept of "hold planet X's ships
this turn so it can fire a
larger strike next turn.
What you observed in gameplay:
You're describing
two distinct things:
1. Early game - all-in expansion: drain each planet fully, trust production to refill. This works because the
safety margin safe_drain holds back is often unnecessary -
your attack succeeds and the planet refills in a few turns anyway.
2. Late game - multi-source coordinated strike: send 0.25 each from several planets at the last enemy holdouts. This works because no single planet has enough ships, but
combined they exceed the floor.
Why the engine misses this:
The greedy planner sees each opportunity independently. If planet A has 30 ships and the enemy has a floor of 40,
it skips that target. It never considers: "what if I
stop sending planet A's ships to random neutrals for 5 turns, let it accumulate to 45, then fire the decisive strike?" That requires multi-turn planning the 1-ply scorer
structurally can't do.
Where this connects to our running work:
This is actually one of the strongest arguments
for the Track C VF that just landed (AUC=0.99). A value function trained on real prize-zone games has seen
this pattern -
that a board state where you have 60
ships concentrated on one planet near a high-value enemy is actually a winning
even if the l-ply greedy scorer
undervalues it because you haven't fired yet. The VF could guide a "hold and concentrate" move that the greedy scorer would never pick.
The stochastic search (192 candidates) also partially captures this - one of those 192 candidates is effectively "don't send
from this planet at all" (attack a target
with low EV), which lets the garrison grow. EV might correctly rank "hold" above
"fire at a weak neutral."
Is there a simpler heuristic version?
Yes, in principle: identify floor-blocked high-value targets, designate the nearest owned planet as a "staging planet," and
suppress its outgoing attacks until garrison
> floor. No rotation math needed, purely garrison-based. The challenge is doing this statelessly (orbit_lite decides fresh each turn) - you'd need to re-identify the
staging intent each turn from the
current observation.
It's buildable.
But given the stochastic search and VF are already running overnight targeting the same underlying problem (candidate expansion + better evaluation), I'd
wait to see their results before adding another experiment. If both fail, this is the natural next thing to try.
* Waiting for 3 background agents to finish

On Ted's message 1 (on-policy constraint):
You're right,
and it reframes the architecture question cleanly. The only live question is whether the PPO critic can be warm-started from the v5 value probe - but Track
"VE NULL RESULT" and there's no value_probe.pt in the repo.
The v5 BC checkpoints in training/ (bc_policy.pt, clone_*.pt) use a different encoder
architecture than the current MLP backbone, so direct weight transfer isn't possible without building an adapter. Given 5 days left, that path isn't worth the cost.
are prioritized
exactly right for this campaign:
Warm-start critic - blocked by architecture mismatch (not worth fixing now)
Feature/reward
calibration - already baked in (OBS_DIM=302 encoding was designed from episode analysis)
3. Held-out calibration set - worth having but the 5-bot gym gauntlet is the current proxy; live LB is ground truth
The opponent pool use is already in the plan:
comet_reaper + schmeekler_fmt enter the pool at U=1000. I'll add
orbit_lite as well (it's the weakest useful opponent, good
for early training signal before the policy can beat either heuristic).
On Ted's message 2 (advantage normalization tension):
The tension is real and worth resolving. ppo_update() at line 440 already normalizes advantages: adv_np = (adv_np - adv_np.mean()) / (adv_np. std() + 1e-8). So why does
reward_scale matter?
The answer is signal density, not signal scale. Normalization can't fix a sparse buffer. With reward_scale=0.001:
- Most buffer entries: ship-advantage delta × 0.001 ~ 0.0 exactly (early policy barely fires, advantage barely moves)
- Rare terminal entries: #1.0 terminal bonus (when game ends in a rollout)
- After normalization: ~99% of buffer has advantage ~ 0,
~1% has ladvantagel >> 0
- CF measures the fraction of all entries where Iratio-1l > 0.2 → dominated by the 99% zeros → CF ~ 0.003
With reward_scale=0.01, each ship launch/capture contributes a non-trivial per-step signal → more buffer entries have non-zero advantage → CF rises
batch, not just the terminal events.
So the 10x bump is the right fix for the current sparsity. Long term, advantage normalization IS the correct invariant and we should keep it - once the policy fires
frequently enough to produce dense ship-advantage deltas, reward_scale becomes genuinely irrelevant and we can remove it.
On the three-metric gate: confirmed. After the H1 fix, passing = CF in 0.05-0.3 AND EV rising off zero AND entropy decaying gradually. EV and entropy are now logged (as
of the last session's update to ppo_update - lines 491-501 in train.py). Will specifically watch for entropy collapse (crash → pathological, should be gradual decay over
hundreds of updates).
Behavioral checks alongside: will run greedy win% and PO/P1 balance as
separate checks - they are NOT substitutes for the health metrics.
On AgileRL: agree
completely,
don't switch.
I don't have context
on what was in
the screenshot beyond "On AgileRL" - if you have the rest of that note, I'd like to see
it, but the conclusion is unchanged regardless: one scalar fix, 5 days left, custom pipeline stays.

• Honest answers to Ted's questions:
Have we proved locally that training is working?
Not definitively yet. Here's the
picture:
H1 test (reward_scale=0.01), now at U=70:
U=10: CF=0.071
(in band -
policy is moving)
U=20: CF=0.048
U=30: CF=0.008
(dropping)
U=50: CF=0.004
U=70: CF=0.005
EP=0
still
CF
gave a genuine
burst early (policy moved away from random init), then dropped as the policy settled. EP=0 through U=70 is actually normal - the original training also
had EP=0 for the first ~100 updates, then EP=48
fired at U=110. Games take ~120-160 updates before the first terminal events. The real test is whether CF picks back up
when terminal rewards start firing (~U=100-120). The eval at U=200 will tell us if the policy learned anything vs greedy (baseline was 25% at reward_scale=0.001).
TL;DR: training is not broken but not confirmed working yet. We need to
see U=100-150 before we know.
Do we need shared storage between Jetstream instances?
No. Each instance trains completely independently - no coordination needed. Each instance saves its best checkpoint to its own local disk.
How does data get back to local?
On demand, using
sync_checkpoints.sh:
bash agents/rl_ppo/sync_checkpoints.sh
This SSHes to each of the 5 instances and pulls best_model.pt from each run directory. We run this every few hours, compare
the checkpoints, and submit the best one.
What's the overall plan:
• deploy_all.sh running → syncing repo
to 5 existing m3.2xl instances
(slow because it's transferring episode replay files - fixed for next deploy)
• H1 local test running → validating reward_scale=0.01 fix through U=300
Once deploy finishes (~10-20 min):
• 20 parallel training runs start on Jetstream2
• Once Ted shares IPs for the 3 new m3. xl → add
to deploy_all.sh → 12 more runs
• Total: 32 runs at ~17K SPS

'clip_frac': 0.05-0.30 (near 0
learning; >0.5 = exploding updates)

should rise steadily toward 0.8+

3 combined heads) and decay
'entropy: should start high (~5.0 for
3 combined heads) and decay
'kl_approx': typically 0.005-0.02 per
'value_loss': should converge from ~0.5 to ~0.05 as
value head fits
over training
over training

• H1 is a RED gate. The local run just completed (it was configured for 300 updates). Results:
U=200 eval vs greedy: 25%
- identical to baseline (reward_scale=0.001)
CF = 0.000 virtually always (spikes to 0.015 only when EP>0)
EV = wildly oscillating (-3.87 to +0.84), not rising
Entropy = flat 5.0 throughout → policy is still uniform random
The reward_scale fix didn't work. Here's why the normalization
tension resolves differently than expected: the inter-step delta is structurally zero, not just small.
delta = (my_ships - enemy_ships)_change.
With two random agents who never fire, ship counts only change from planet production - a near-constant drift that
cancels out
in the delta. Multiplying zero by 10 is still zero. Normalization then divides a buffer of zeros by a tiny std and amplifies noise, which is why EV oscillates wildly
(critic is fitting noise).
Root
cause is the cold-start problem, not reward
agents start random → neither fires → no
→ no ship loss
signal → delta ~ 0 always → only terminal
events carry signal (EP=0 for 8+ consecutive updates, then EP=50 for one update) → policy has nothing to learn between terminals → 25% eval (below random in 2P, which
means fire head is collapsed to never-fire).
The 32 remote jobs started 5 minutes ago and are running the identical broken config.
The fix is minimal - add the greedy agent as a fixed P1 opponent from step 0, so it fires at us immediately and creates combat signal:
# In train.py, after opponent_pool = OpponentPool()
* Seed the pool with a frozen greedy so P1 fires from update 1
pponent_pool.add_fixed(greedy_agent) # or just hardcode P1 = greedy for first 500 updates
What I want to line up while remote jobs are young enough to kill and restart:
1. Fix train.py - P1 starts as greedy,
switches to self-play mix once pool has checkpoints
2. Kill remote jobs + deploy the fix before they waste hours on a dead
config
Should I implement the greedy-opponent fix and redeploy? The remote jobs are at U~1 right now.
* Sock-hopping... (3m 36s • + 8.0k tokens)
The big news in this screenshot is the one line that should make you exhale: H1 is **confirmed by data, not just by reasoning**. CF=0 at U=380 with the old
'reward_scale versus CF=0.052 at U=91 with 0.01. That's the falsifiable prediction from last turn coming true,
and faster than expected - it's already entering band at
91 updates instead of needing 300. The signal-density diagnosis was right, the scalar fix worked, and the actor is now learning.
That's a real milestone. The framework
caught the dead-actor pathology in the first 200 local updates
instead of after 20 remote job-hours, which is exactly the value it was built to deliver.
That said, read CF=0.052 precisely: it's at the very bottom edge of the 0.05-0.3 band, and it's one metric.
band" is not yet "green gate." The gate you and
the orchestrator agreed on is three metrics plus two behavioral checks, and right now you have one metric at its floor. Before this counts as passed, you still need EV
rising off zero, entropy decaying gradually (not collapsing), and the PO/P1 balance and greedy-win checks. The orchestrator's own table reflects this honestly - H2 (РО
systematic advantage) is still "Pending (after gate green)." Good that it's tracked; just don't let the excitement of CF moving collapse the distinction between "the
actor is learning" and "the gate is green."

• Now answering Ted's question about training against comet_reaper:
Yes, it's a good idea, and it's better than pure
self-play or
greedy. Here's why it works well:
comet_reaper fires
aggressively from turn 1, which immediately gives
the RL policy combat signal - ship losses, ship gains
from kills, real
delta in the
dense reward.
Pure self-play had
zero combat signal because both
random and never
Greedy fires,
comet_reaper fires
and uses intercept geometry and fleet
management, so the RL policy has to learn counter-play that's actually relevant to what it will face at submission time.
The one risk is that comet_reaper is so strong that the early RL policy gets crushed every episode and
the value function just learns "state = losing". But in practice
this is fine because:
- The terminal reward is still +1/-1 (correctly signed)
- The dense reward (ship
advantage delta) gives
every step, not just at
- PPO will find any action that reduces the loss margin - even
small improvements register as positive advantages
The alternative concern - "curriculum starting from easy then graduating to hard" - matters when the gap is so large
the policy gets zero gradient. Here we have dense
ship-advantage shaping so there's gradient even from losing positions.
One practical thing: comet_reaper uses orbit_lite and torch, so the remote machines need the orbit_lite path accessible. Let me verify the import will work from train.py
before deploying:

On your three questions:
Sync between instances - they don't share state in real-time, and that's intentional. 32 jobs with 32 different random seeds = 32 independent explorations of the policy
space. Diversity is a feature, not a bug. The orchestration pattern is: sync_checkpoints.sh pulls the best checkpoint from every instance to your local machine every 10
min. When a model clearly beats the gate, you (or me, acting as orchestrator) push it back as the new shared opponent
for the next generation. That's the coordination
Is the game code correct? Yes - kaggle-environments runs the actual competition engine, the same binary that evaluates submissions on the leaderboard. Our observation
encoding (302-dim) maps real game state. The GAE fix confirmed the policy IS learning: CF went from 0.003 → 0.062, entropy is decaying,
and the value head is fitting (EV
early is good - dense reward from comet_reaper attacks). Whether the final policy beats comet_reaper's 1234.7 ELO floor is the open question, but the
machinery is correct.
What you're orchestrating right now: 1 local run (gae_full, U=200
eval ~30 min away) + 32 remote jobs on 8 instances
all with the GAE fix. Gate flips green if gae_full
beats 25% vs greedy at U=200.

On AgileRL: The problem isn't pPO
code quality - it's that our action space is non-standard. Per-planet heads (fire/target/fraction per owned planet) don't fit any
framework's standard Box or Discrete space. AgileRL,
SB3, CleanRL - all of them assume fixed action dimensions. We'd spend 1-2 days writing the gym wrapper and adapting
the multi-head architecture, and we'd be right back to the same learning challenge with 3 fewer days.
The real question - are we learning? We'll know in about 7 minutes when eval_fix_test hits U=100 with a working eval
for the first time. That's the first honest signal
we've ever gotten. Let's see it before deciding anything.

Our bottleneck is
the game engine,
not the neural network.
Every training step looks like this:
[Step 1]
Run 64
game environments → generate observations
[Step
Neural net forward pass → get actions
[Step 3]
PPO gradient update
The orbit-wars game runs
in Python via kaggle-environments.
by how fast the game engine runs, not by how fast PyTorch is.
A GPU would speed up steps 2 and 3, which together are maybe 5% of wall
time. So:
for a GPU slot? -- it doesnt' matter if we use cpu or gpu? I don't
- PYTHON/CPU,
~95% of time
tiny,
217K params, ~2% of
time
+ ~3% of time
be moved
to a GPU - it's just a Python simulation running on CPU cores. Our 65 SPS ceiling is set
Hardware
m3.2x1
(64 CPU) now
Anvil GPU node
(128 CPU + A100)
Anvil CPU node (128 CPU)
Expected SPS
~65
~70-80 (GPU barely helps)
~130 (2x CPUs = 2x parallel envs)
More CPU cores = more parallel game environments = linear SPS
GPU = almost no benefit for us.
So: CPU nodes now, don't wait for GPU. One Anvil wholenode (128 CPUs)
beats any GPU node for our workload,
and there are 93
available right now vs

* Churned for 4m 58s • 2 shells still running
So we can keep updating the training script? It's not like it invalidates previous training data or do we start from zero any time we make changes
• Great question - and the honest answer is every restart we've
done tonight has been from zero.
pkill + relaunch = random initialized weights each time.
The
best_model.pt
gets
saved
during a run, but the new run doesn't load it - it starts fresh.
Was that the right call? Yes, because all three
fixed were fundamental:
- GAE bug → advantages were wrong, policy couldn't learn → weights from that run were
- ent_coef bug → policy rewarded for being random → weights were garbage
- eval bug → didn't affect training, only measurement
Starting fresh with correct code beats resuming a broken policy.
Going forward this is a real problem though. Now that the code
is correct, every time we touch train.py and restart we throw away accumulated learning. At 63 sec/update
on the remotes, throwing away 200 updates = 3.5 hours of compute gone.
The fix is checkpoint resume - add ~10 lines to train():
# Start of train(), after policy is created:
if args.resume and os.path.exists(os.path.join(run_dir,
"best_model.pt")):
ckpt = torch.load(os.path.join(run_dir,
"best_model.pt"))
policy.load_state_dict(ckpt["state"])
start_update = ckpt["update"]
Resumed from U{start_update}")
Then --resume flag lets you restart a job and pick up where it left off. Changes to eval code, logging, hyperparameters all become non-destructive.
Want me to add that now? It's the most important infrastructure piece before we let the fleet run overnight - if a Jetstream instance reboots or the job dies, we
don'+
want to lose 8+ hours of training.

RL training code audit
› You are an expert in deep reinforcement learning, specifically PPO (Proximal Policy Optimization). Your job
suggest style improvements or refactors - only fla

OCR TEXT:
• Build orchestrator for RL training arena
0 $2
TH2
ORCHESTRATOR
AUDITOR
JETSTREAM FLEET
- H3
..rc/orbit_wars (-zsh)
RL training code audit
› You are an expert in deep reinforcement learning, specifically PPO (Proximal Policy Optimization). Your job
suggest style improvements or refactors - only flag bugs that would cause the policy to fail to learn or learn the wrong
audit of a PPO
training implementation.
Do
a training restart from
zero.
1. Advantage normalization done globally over the whole buffer, not per-mini-batch (line 474)
# line 474 - before the epoch loop
adv_np = (adv_np - adv_np.mean()) / (adv_np.std() + 1e-8)
Normalizing once over the full buffer before any mini-batch loop is the standard CleanRL/Stable-Baselines approach and is technically acceptable - it is NOT
as normalizing per-mini-batch (which is actually less common). However,
the way the code is structured,
the normalized
array is then
per mini-batch
each epoch. Because the permutation idx is recomputed each epoch (line 482) and the
same pre-normalized adv_np is used, this
is actually correct: advantages are
normalized once, then shuffled identically each epoch. This is not a bug. (No action needed.)
2. GAE bootstrap uses vals[t+1]
from the buffer instead
of a true next-state value (line 579)
nv = 0.0 if dones[t] or t == T - 1 else vals[t + 17
At t == T - 1 (the last step in the rollout), nv = 0.0 is used, which is wrong
for non-terminal states. The last observation's value is simply discarded. The correct PPO
bootstrap is:
nv = 0.0 if dones[t] else (0.0 if t == T - 1 else vals[t + 1])
Wait - that's identical. The real issue is subtler: vals[t] is the value at step t as estimated during rollout collection, but after a done at step t, the environment is
immediately reset (line 735), and new_obs_p0[env_i] is replaced with the fresh episode's first observation. The exp["val"] stored for the planet-decisions at step t
correctly came from the pre-done observation, so vals[t] for a terminal step is correct. And nv = 0.0 for a done is correct.
The t == T-1 clause cuts off bootstrapping
at the rollout boundary, setting the value of the last state to
zero even if the episode is still running. This is the actual bug.
Impact: For all non-terminal last-step transitions, GAE pretends the future value is zero. Every 64th step in every environment, the advantage estimate is wrong by gamma
* V(s_{T}). With 64 rollout steps and 64 envs, roughly 1/64 of all buffer entries have corrupted advantages. This is a persistent bias toward underestimating future
value at rollout boundaries - it will cause the policy to under-value long-horizon play and the critic to train on wrong targets.
Fix: After the rollout loop, collect one additional value estimate for the last obs of each env (the "bootstrap value"), then use it in GAE:
# After rollout collection, before compute_gae:
# Store bootstrap values keyed by env_i in the buffer or pass separately.
# In compute_gae, for t == T-1 and not done:
nv = bootstrap_val[env_i] # V(s_{T}) from a no-grad forward pass
This requires storing last_obs_p0 after the rollout loop and doing one forward pass per env. This is the single most impactful missing piece in the GAE implementation.
3. prev_adv_pO not updated on non-terminal steps - shaped reward references stale ship counts (lines 727-730, 738)

JETSTREAM FLEET
we have 5x 64 cores, and 3x 32 cores, and 1x 4 cores, why aren't we using all of them to get more throughput in training?
why wouldn't we use all the cores? if the envs are sequentials, .. if you're saying most cores are staying idle, why don't we use them?
• Exactly right - that's the bug we're fixing right now. The deployment is running in background. Here's the math:
Old setup:
- 5x m3. 2x1 (64 cores each): 4 jobs = 4 of 64 cores
3x m3.xl (32 cores each): 4 jobs = 4 of 32 cores used
- Total: ~2,100 steps/sec
New setup (deploying now):
- 5x m3.2x1: 16 jobs x 16 envs = 16 of 64 cores used (25%)
- 3x m3. xl: 8 jobs x 16 envs = 8 of 32 cores used (25%)
- 1x m3. quad: 4 jobs × 8 envs = 4 of 4 cores (100%)
- Total: ~6,960 steps/sec - 3x more throughput, same hardware
We could push further (32 jobs per 2x1 = 50% cores), but below 16 envs per job the gradient batches get noisy. 16 jobs × 16
envs is
a reasonable tradeoff.
The underlying reason we weren't using all cores: Python's GIL means each training job is single-threaded. The only way to use more
cores is more jobs. The environments
run sequentially inside each job - so num_envs=64 still only uses 1 core.
Read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)
• Background command "Scale fleet to use more cores: 16 jobs on 2xl, 8 on x1, 4 on quad" completed (exit code 0)
• All confirmed:
- 5x m3. 2x1: 16 jobs each v
- 3x m3.xl: 8 jobs each v
- m3. quad: venv rebuilt, 4 jobs running ~
Total: 108 jobs (was 36). At ~65 SPS each that's roughly 7,000 steps/sec vs the previous 2,100.
The fleet is now doing ~3x the work on the same hardware. First evals at U=100 will land in ~27 minutes - that's your proof-of-concept moment. Want me to
set a wakeup to
report those results?

Good news for the fleet: the disease
is NOT fleet-wide. The Auditor's diagnostic was EV@U=1 = 0.95 = broken. The m3.2xl/m3.xl fleet reads 0.70-0.80, in the same range as the working
eval_fix_test run (0.626). The fleet's 32 jobs are not burning on broken code.
The m3. quad IS the disease case: EV@U=1 = 0.90-0.96. Combined with entropy=2.93 at U=79 and fast collapse, the quad is exhibiting exactly the pattern the auditor described. Root cause:
--num_envs 8 produces too-small batches, return variance collapses, value fits trivially.
v6_greedy local collapse: entropy=0.55, CF=0.000, eval=12%. This is the same low-batch signal problem - the local
faster but the rollout batch at num_envs=64, rollout_steps=64 =
4096 steps may still produce low-variance returns against greedy (greedy games are close, delta is small). Not enough evidence yet to call this a code regression vs the
Recommended actions:
1. Don't kill the fleet.
EV@U=1 = 0.70-0.80 is healthy. Let it run to U=100
eval (~55 min) to confirm.
2. Kill or relaunch m3. quad with num_envs=64. The quad at 4 cores with 8 envs is producing the disease. If you want to keep it, set --num_envs 64 - it'll be
slow (SPS~16) but the batch
will be large enough. Or repurpose as dashboard host.
3. Submit Producer Hybrid v8 immediately. You dropped the full,
tested main.py.
All smoke tests pass,
This is a proven orbit_lite engine with data-driven v8 fixes (opening

ORCHESTRATOR
ORBIT WARS

* 983
The Auditor is raising the right alarm but the failure mode is different from what they described.
Here's the actual data across all 32 64-env seeds:
CF trajectory: U=50 → U=100 → U=150 → U=200
UNIVERSAL: EV shoots to 0.96-0.99 by U=100 on 31/32 seeds
CF at U=200 distribution:
CF ≤ 0.010:
O seeds
(none dead yet)
CF 0.011-0.030:
8 seeds
(borderline - watch)
CF 0.031-0.060: 17 seeds (working)
CF > 0.060:
7 seeds (healthy)
EXCEPTION: m3.x1-1 j1 → EV=0.742 at U=200 (not saturated), CF=0.073
The advantage normalization mitigates the EV>CF chain: Line 504 in train.py normalizes advantages to zero mean / unit variance before every PPO update.
So even though EV→0.97 (critic
claims confidence), the NORMALIZED advantages have unit variance and the policy keeps getting a gradient signal. This is why CF hasn't gone to 0 - it's bouncing in the 0.02-0.07 range
(real updates happening).
The actual failure mode killing vo_greedy was entropy collapse, not advantage collapse. V6_greedy at U=2900: ENT=0.694, CF=0.000.
With normalized advantages, CF→0 can only happen if the
policy goes deterministic CENT collapses, all log-ratios → 0, ratios ~ 1, no clipping). That's entropy collapse, not advantage extinction.
Current fleet entropy status:
Seed depth U=200:
ENT 2.0-3.0: 5 seeds - moderate decay (includes the 31% star at ENT=2.48)
ENT > 3.0: 26 seeds - still plenty of exploration headroom
"5-alarm fire"
for the 64-env fleet right now. 0/32 seeds have CF=0. The fleet at U=200 is NOT a "mostly dead policies" situation. It's a "moderate learning, heterogeneous
1. EV saturating at U=100 on 31/32
seeds is a sign the critic has little more to learn - future training is driven by noisy normalized advantages, which increases gradient variance
8 seeds with CF 0.011-0.030 are at risk if entropy collapses before the policy finds a good equilibrium
3. The v6_greedy trajectory tells us entropy CAN collapse before U=1000, potentially before the policy ever reaches 30%+ eval
The right diagnostic for the next 6 hours: Track ENT trajectory at U=300
eval results.
collapsing fast (e.g., hitting ENT<1.0 by U=300), we need to increase ent_coef to
slow it down.
continues decaying normally (2.0-3.0 → 1.0-2.0 by U=400), fleet is on track.
If ENT is
One action I'm taking now: The RL vs RL
eval bug is fixed (eval_checkpoints.py). The champion loop can now run real promotion evals instead of silent crashes. This matters because if we
wait for perfect training, we'll never promote a champion.

* Crunched for 3m 57s • 2 shells still running
* recap: Running a PPO self-play fleet to beat comet_reaper before the June 23 deadline; the RL model beats greedy at 37% but 0% vs comet across all seeds.
One remaining candidate fires its
U=500 eval at ~01:30 MT - if it scores 40%,
it gets a promotion trial.
what's the strategic outlook - should we keep running or shut down?
• Shut down. The comet signal is definitive.
Here's why:
The 0% vs comet_reaper is categorical, not noise. Five seeds, depths U=300 through U=500,
20 games each - O wins total. That's roughly 100 games played against comet across the fleet and
not a single win. At true WR ~0%, you'd need exceptional variance
explain that. This isn't "early training, give it
time" - the policy has converged somewhere that beats greedy (~37%)
but is categorically dominated by comet_reaper's heuristics.
The greedy-vs-comet gap is structural. Beating a greedy opponent means learning basic positional play. Beating comet_reaper requires understanding its specific orbit-capture strategy. The
RL agent isn't acquiring that. After ZM steps it's still at 0%. Another ZM steps won't bridge a structural representational gap.
The only remaining shot is a coin flip. j2 at U=500 (~01:30 MT)
12/30 wins (40%) to
save a new best_model.
is probably 37%. With n=30, that's roughly a 30-35% chance of
hitting 40%. And even if it does - it would promote a model that still beats comet_reaper 0% of the time.
The champion wouldn't actually improve.
The urgent action isn't fleet shutdown - it's resubmission.
Schmeekler_fmt (~1149
Elo) is currently your SELECTED submission.
Comet_reaper (~1235) is not submitted. That's an 86-Elo
just sitting there. Every day you wait with the wrong bot selected is a missed opportunity.
My recommendation:
1. Kill the fleet now (or let it idle - Jetstream2 cost is sunk)
2. Re-submit comet_reaper TODAY, not June 22
- that trigger was a safety net, not a target
3. RL track is closed: 0% vs comet at U=500 is the verdict
Should I queue up the comet_reaper resubmission steps, or do you want to pull the lever yourself?
